<p>During the night of 14/15 April, a diversionary landing was planned near <a href="/wiki/Baracoa" title="Baracoa">Baracoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oriente_Province" title="Oriente Province">Oriente Province</a>, by about 164 Cuban exiles commanded by Higinio 'Nino' Diaz. Their mother ship, named <i>La Playa</i> or <i>Santa Ana</i>, had sailed from Key West under a Costa Rican ensign. Several US Navy destroyers were stationed offshore near <a href="/wiki/Guant%C3%A1namo_Bay" title="Guantánamo Bay">Guantánamo Bay</a> to give the appearance of an impending invasion fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The reconnaissance boats turned back to the ship after their crews detected activities by Cuban militia forces along the coastline.<sup id="cite_ref-szulc1986_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-szulc1986-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnson_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999156–59_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999156–59-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-triay_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-triay-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of those activities, at daybreak, a <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a> sortie over the Baracoa area was launched from Santiago de Cuba. That was a FAR (<a href="/wiki/Cuban_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuban Air Force">Fuerza Aérea Revolucionaria</a>)  (Cuban Air Force) T-33, piloted by Lt Orestes Acosta, and it crashed fatally into the sea. On 17 April, his name was falsely quoted as a defector among the disinformation circulating in Miami.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The CIA, with the backing of the Pentagon, had originally requested permission to produce sonic booms over Havana on 14 April to create an air of confusion.  The request was a form of psychological warfare that had proven successful in the overthrow of Jacobo Arbenz in Guatemala in 1954.  The point was to create confusion in Havana and have it be a distraction to Castro if they could "break all the windows in town."<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The request was denied, however, since officials thought such would be too obvious a sign of involvement by the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-nsarchive.gwu.edu_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsarchive.gwu.edu-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 April 1961, at about 06:00 am Cuban local time, eight Douglas <a href="/wiki/A-26_Invader" class="mw-redirect" title="A-26 Invader">B-26B Invader</a> bombers in three groups simultaneously attacked three Cuban airfields at <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio_de_los_Ba%C3%B1os" title="San Antonio de los Baños">San Antonio de los Baños</a> and at Ciudad Libertad (formerly named Campo Columbia), both near Havana, plus the <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Maceo_Airport" title="Antonio Maceo Airport">Antonio Maceo International Airport</a> at Santiago de Cuba. The B-26s had been prepared by the CIA on behalf of Brigade 2506, and had been painted with the <a href="/wiki/False_flag" title="False flag">false flag</a> markings of the FAR, the air force of the Cuban government. Each came armed with bombs, rockets, and machine guns. They had flown from <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Cabezas" title="Puerto Cabezas">Puerto Cabezas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, and were crewed by exiled Cuban pilots and navigators of the self-styled <i>Fuerza Aérea de Liberación</i> (FAL). The purpose of the action (code-named <i>Operation Puma</i>) was reportedly to destroy most or all of the armed aircraft of the FAR in preparation for the main invasion. At Santiago, the two attackers destroyed a <a href="/wiki/C-47" class="mw-redirect" title="C-47">C-47</a> transport, a <a href="/wiki/PBY_Catalina" class="mw-redirect" title="PBY Catalina">PBY Catalina</a> flying boat, two B-26s and a civilian <a href="/wiki/DC-3" class="mw-redirect" title="DC-3">DC-3</a> plus various other civilian aircraft. At San Antonio, the three attackers destroyed three FAR B-26s, one Sea Fury and one T-33, and one attacker diverted to <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Grand Cayman</a> due to low usable fuel. Aircraft that diverted to the <a href="/wiki/Caymans" class="mw-redirect" title="Caymans">Caymans</a> was aggressively seized by Great Britain since they were suspicious that the Cayman Islands might be perceived as a launch site for the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-nsarchive.gwu.edu_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsarchive.gwu.edu-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> At Ciudad Libertad, the three attackers destroyed only non-operational aircraft such as two <a href="/wiki/P-47" class="mw-redirect" title="P-47">P-47</a> Thunderbolts. One of those attackers was damaged by anti-aircraft fire, and ditched about 50&#160;km north of Cuba,<sup id="cite_ref-shono_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shono-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> with the loss of its crew Daniel Fernández Mon and Gaston Pérez. Its companion B-26, also damaged, continued north and landed at Boca Chica field (<a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Key_West" title="Naval Air Station Key West">Naval Air Station Key West</a>), Florida. The crew, José Crespo and Lorenzo Pérez-Lorenzo, were granted political asylum, and made their way back to <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> the next day via Miami and the daily CIA C-54 flight from <a href="/wiki/Opa-locka_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Opa-locka Airport">Opa-locka Airport</a> to <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Cabezas_Airport" title="Puerto Cabezas Airport">Puerto Cabezas Airport</a>. Their B-26, purposely numbered 933, the same as at least two other B-26s that day for disinformation reasons, was held until late on 17 April.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999130_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999130-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Deception_flight_.28Saturday.2C_15_April.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Deception_flight_(Saturday,_15_April)">Deception flight (Saturday, 15 April)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Deception flight (Saturday, 15 April)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>About 90 minutes after the eight B-26s had taken off from Puerto Cabezas to attack Cuban airfields, another B-26 departed on a deception flight that took it close to Cuba but headed north towards Florida. Like the bomber groups, it carried false FAR markings and the same number 933 as painted on at least two of the others. Before departure, the cowling from one of the aircraft's two engines was removed by CIA personnel, fired upon, then re-installed to give the false appearance that the aircraft had taken ground fire at some point during its flight. At a safe distance north of Cuba, the pilot feathered the engine with the pre-installed bullet holes in the cowling, radioed a <a href="/wiki/Mayday_(distress_signal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayday (distress signal)">mayday</a> call, and requested immediate permission to land at Miami International airport.  He landed and taxied to the military area of the airport near an Air Force C-47 and was met by several government cars. The pilot was Mario Zúñiga, formerly of the FAEC (Cuban Air Force under Batista), and after landing, he masqueraded as 'Juan Garcia' and publicly claimed that three colleagues had also defected from the FAR. The next day he was granted political asylum, and that night he returned to Puerto Cabezas via Opa-Locka.<sup id="cite_ref-hagedorn2006_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hagedorn2006-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> This deception operation was successful at the time in convincing much of the world media that the attacks on the Cuban Air Force bases were the work of an internal anti-Communist faction, and did not involve outside actors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200920–21_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200920–21-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reactions_.2815_April.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions_(15_April)">Reactions (15 April)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reactions (15 April)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At 10:30 am on 15 April at the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the Cuban Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%BAl_Roa_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Raúl Roa García">Raúl Roa</a> accused the US of aggressive air attacks against Cuba, and that afternoon formally tabled a motion to the Political (First) Committee of the UN General Assembly. Only days earlier, the CIA had unsuccessfully attempted to entice Raúl Roa into defecting.<sup id="cite_ref-nsarchive.gwu.edu_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsarchive.gwu.edu-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In response to Roa's accusations before the UN, US ambassador to the UN <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a> stated that US armed forces would not "under any conditions" intervene in Cuba, and that the US would do everything in its power to ensure that no US citizens would participate in actions against Cuba. He also stated that Cuban defectors had carried out the attacks that day, and he presented a UPI wire photo of Zúñiga's B-26 in Cuban markings at Miami airport.<sup id="cite_ref-nsa_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Stevenson was later embarrassed to realize that the CIA had lied to him.<sup id="cite_ref-faria_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faria-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President Kennedy supported the statement made by Stevenson: "I have emphasized before that this was a struggle of Cuban patriots against a Cuban dictator. While we could not be expected to hide our sympathies, we made it repeatedly clear that the armed forces of this country would not intervene in any way".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 April, the Cuban national police, led by <a href="/wiki/Efigenio_Ameijeiras" title="Efigenio Ameijeiras">Efigenio Ameijeiras</a>, started the process of arresting thousands of suspected anti-revolutionary individuals, and detaining them in provisional locations such as the Karl Marx Theatre, the moat of <a href="/wiki/La_Caba%C3%B1a" title="La Cabaña">Fortaleza de la Cabana</a> and the Principe Castle all in Havana, and the baseball park in Matanzas.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Phony_war_.2816_April.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Phony_war_(16_April)">Phony war (16 April)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phony war (16 April)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On the night of 15/16 April, the Nino Diaz group failed in a second attempted diversionary landing at a different location near Baracoa.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 16 April, Merardo Leon, Jose Leon, and 14 others staged an armed uprising at Las Delicias Estate in Las Villas, with only four surviving. Leonel Martinez and three others took to the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-corzo_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corzo-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2009)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Following the air strikes on the Cuban airfields on 15 April 1961, the FAR managed to prepare for armed action with its surviving air force which numbered at least four T-33s jet trainers, four Sea Furies fighters and five or six B-26 medium bombers. All three types were armed with machine guns (a 20mm cannon, in the case of the Sea Furies) for air-to-air combat and for strafing of ships and ground targets. CIA planners had failed to discover that the US-supplied T-33 trainer jets had long been armed with M-3 machine guns. The three types could also carry bombs and rocket pods for attacks against ships and tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-macphall_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macphall-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>No additional air strikes against Cuban airfields and aircraft were specifically planned before 17 April, because B-26 pilots' exaggerated claims gave the CIA false confidence in the success of 15 April attacks, until <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2</a> reconnaissance photos taken on 16 April showed otherwise. Late on 16 April, President Kennedy ordered cancellation of further airfield strikes planned for dawn on 17 April, to attempt plausible deniability of US direct involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-kornbluh_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kornbluh-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Late on 16 April, the CIA/<a href="/wiki/Brigade_2506" title="Brigade 2506">Brigade 2506</a> invasion fleet converged on 'Rendezvous Point Zulu', about 65 kilometres (40&#160;mi) south of Cuba, having sailed from <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Cabezas" title="Puerto Cabezas">Puerto Cabezas</a> in Nicaragua where they had been loaded with troops and other <a href="/wiki/Materiel" title="Materiel">materiel</a>, after loading arms and supplies at New Orleans. The US Navy operation was code-named <i>Bumpy Road</i>, having been changed from <i>Crosspatch</i> on 1 April 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-kornbluh_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kornbluh-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The fleet, labeled the 'Cuban Expeditionary Force' (CEF), included five 2,400-ton (empty weight) freighter ships chartered by the CIA from the Garcia Line, and subsequently outfitted with anti-aircraft guns. Four of the freighters, <i>Houston</i> (code name <i>Aguja</i>), <i>Río Escondido</i> (code name <i>Ballena</i>), <i>Caribe</i> (code name <i>Sardina</i>), and <i>Atlántico</i> (code-name <i>Tiburón</i>), were planned to transport about 1,400 troops in seven battalions of troops and armaments near to the invasion beaches. The fifth freighter, <i>Lake Charles</i>, was loaded with follow-up supplies and some <a href="/wiki/Operation_40" title="Operation 40">Operation 40</a> infiltration personnel. The freighters sailed under Liberian ensigns. Accompanying them were two LCIs (<a href="/wiki/Landing_Craft_Infantry" title="Landing Craft Infantry">Landing Craft Infantry</a>) outfitted with heavy armament at Key West. The LCIs were <i>Blagar</i> (code-name <i>Marsopa</i>) and <i>Barbara J</i> (code-name <i>Barracuda</i>), sailing under Nicaraguan ensigns. After exercises and training at Vieques Island, the CEF ships were individually escorted (outside visual range) to <i>Point Zulu</i> by US Navy destroyers <a href="/wiki/USS_Bache_(DD-470)" title="USS Bache (DD-470)">USS&#160;<i>Bache</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Beale_(DD-471)" title="USS Beale (DD-471)">USS&#160;<i>Beale</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Conway_(DD-507)" title="USS Conway (DD-507)">USS&#160;<i>Conway</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Cony_(DDE-508)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Cony (DDE-508)">USS&#160;<i>Cony</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Eaton_(DD-510)" title="USS Eaton (DD-510)">USS&#160;<i>Eaton</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Murray_(DD-576)" title="USS Murray (DD-576)">USS&#160;<i>Murray</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/USS_Waller_(DDE-466)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Waller (DDE-466)">USS&#160;<i>Waller</i></a>. US Navy Task Group 81.8 had already assembled off the Cayman Islands, commanded by Rear Admiral John E. Clark onboard aircraft carrier USS <i>Essex</i>, plus helicopter assault carrier <a href="/wiki/USS_Boxer_(CV-21)" title="USS Boxer (CV-21)">USS&#160;<i>Boxer</i></a>, destroyers <a href="/wiki/USS_Hank_(DD-702)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Hank (DD-702)">USS&#160;<i>Hank</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_John_W._Weeks_(DD-701)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS John W. Weeks (DD-701)">USS&#160;<i>John W. Weeks</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Purdy_(DD-734)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Purdy (DD-734)">USS&#160;<i>Purdy</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Wren_(DD-568)" title="USS Wren (DD-568)">USS&#160;<i>Wren</i></a>, and submarines <a href="/wiki/USS_Cobbler_(SS-344)" title="USS Cobbler (SS-344)">USS&#160;<i>Cobbler</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Threadfin_(SS-410)" title="USS Threadfin (SS-410)">USS&#160;<i>Threadfin</i></a>. Command and control ship <a href="/wiki/USS_Northampton_(CLC-1)" title="USS Northampton (CLC-1)">USS&#160;<i>Northampton</i></a> and carrier <a href="/wiki/USS_Shangri-La_(CVA-38)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Shangri-La (CVA-38)">USS&#160;<i>Shangri-La</i></a> were also reportedly active in the Caribbean at the time. <a href="/wiki/USS_San_Marcos_(LSD-25)" title="USS San Marcos (LSD-25)">USS&#160;<i>San Marcos</i></a> was a <a href="/wiki/Landing_Ship_Dock" class="mw-redirect" title="Landing Ship Dock">Landing Ship Dock</a> that carried three LCUs (<a href="/wiki/Landing_Craft_Utility" title="Landing Craft Utility">Landing Craft Utility</a>) which could accommodate the Brigades M41 Walker Bulldog tanks and four <a href="/wiki/LCVP_(United_States)" title="LCVP (United States)">LCVPs</a> (Landing Craft, Vehicles, Personnel). <i>San Marcos</i> had sailed from Vieques Island. At <i>Point Zulu</i>, the seven CEF ships sailed north without the USN escorts, except for <i>San Marcos</i> that continued until the seven landing craft were unloaded when just outside the 5 kilometres (3&#160;mi) Cuban territorial limit.<sup id="cite_ref-fernandez_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fernandez-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nsa_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion">Invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Invasion_day_.2817_April.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion_day_(17_April)">Invasion day (17 April)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Invasion day (17 April)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BahiadeCochinos40b.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/BahiadeCochinos40b.png/220px-BahiadeCochinos40b.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/BahiadeCochinos40b.png/330px-BahiadeCochinos40b.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/BahiadeCochinos40b.png/440px-BahiadeCochinos40b.png 2x" data-file-width="1226" data-file-height="826" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BahiadeCochinos40b.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs" title="Bay of Pigs">Bahia de Cochinos</a> 1961</div></div></div>
<p>During the night of 16/17 April, a mock diversionary landing was organized by CIA operatives near <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%ADa_Honda,_Cuba" title="Bahía Honda, Cuba">Bahía Honda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinar_del_R%C3%ADo_Province" title="Pinar del Río Province">Pinar del Río Province</a>. A flotilla containing equipment that broadcast sounds and other effects of a shipborne invasion landing provided the source of Cuban reports that briefly lured Fidel Castro away from the Bay of Pigs battlefront area.<sup id="cite_ref-fernandez_40-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fernandez-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999183_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999183-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 00:00 on 17 April 1961, the two CIA LCIs <i>Blagar</i> and <i>Barbara J</i>, each with a CIA 'operations officer' and an Underwater Demolition Team (UDT) of five frogmen, entered the Bay of Pigs <i>(Bahía de Cochinos)</i> on the southern coast of Cuba. They headed a force of four transport ships (<i>Houston</i>, <i>Río Escondido</i>, <i>Caribe</i> and <i>Atlántico</i>) carrying about 1,400 Cuban exile ground troops of Brigade 2506, plus the Brigade's <a href="/wiki/M41_Walker_Bulldog" title="M41 Walker Bulldog">M41 Walker Bulldog</a> tanks,<sup id="cite_ref-weaponsandwarfare.com_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weaponsandwarfare.com-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and other vehicles in the landing craft.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200924–25_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200924–25-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> At about 01:00, <i>Blagar</i>, as the battlefield command ship, directed the principal landing at Playa Girón (code-named <i>Blue Beach</i>), led by the frogmen in rubber boats followed by troops from <i>Caribe</i> in small aluminium boats, then <a href="/wiki/LCVP_(United_States)" title="LCVP (United States)">LCVPs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Landing_Craft_Utility" title="Landing Craft Utility">LCUs</a> with the M41 Walker Bulldog tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <i>Barbara J</i>, leading <i>Houston</i>, similarly landed troops 35&#160;km further northwest at Playa Larga (code-named <i>Red Beach</i>), using small fiberglass boats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The unloading of troops at night was delayed, due to engine failures and boats damaged by unforeseen coral reefs; the CIA had originally believed that the coral reef was seaweed. As the frogmen came in, they were shocked to discover that the Red Beach was lit with floodlights, which led to the location of the landing being hastily changed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> As the frogmen landed, a firefight broke out when a jeep carrying Cuban militia happened by.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The few militias in the area succeeded in warning Cuban armed forces via radio soon after the first landing, before the invaders overcame their token resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999161,_167_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999161,_167-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Castro was woken up at about 3:15 am to be informed of the landings, which led him to put all militia units in the area on the highest state of alert and to order airstrikes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The Cuban regime planned to strike the <i>brigadistas</i> at Playa Larga first as they were inland before turning on the <i>brigadistas</i> at Girón at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <i>El Comandante</i> departed personally to lead his forces into battle against the <i>brigadistas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At daybreak around 06:30 am, three FAR Sea Furies, one <a href="/wiki/Douglas_A-26_Invader" title="Douglas A-26 Invader">B-26</a> bomber and two <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_T-33" title="Lockheed T-33">Lockheed T-33</a> fighter jets started attacking those CEF ships still unloading troops. At about 06:50, south of Playa Larga, <i>Houston</i> was damaged by several bombs and rockets from a Sea Fury and a T-33, and about two hours later captain Luis Morse intentionally beached it on the western side of the bay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> About 270 troops had been unloaded, but about 180 survivors who struggled ashore were incapable of taking part in further action because of the loss of most of their weapons and equipment. The loss of <i>Houston</i> was a great blow to the <i>brigadistas</i> as that ship was carrying much of the Brigade 2506's medical supplies, which meant that wounded <i>brigadistas</i> had to make do with inadequate medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925_137-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200925-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> At about 07:00, two invading FAL B-26s attacked and sank the Cuban Navy Patrol Escort ship <i>El Baire</i> at <a href="/wiki/Nueva_Gerona" title="Nueva Gerona">Nueva Gerona</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isla_de_la_Juventud" title="Isla de la Juventud">Isle of Pines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> They then proceeded to Girón to join two other B-26s to attack Cuban ground troops and provide distraction air cover for the paratroop C-46s and the CEF ships under air attack. Brigade 2506's M41 Walker Bulldog tanks had all landed by 7:30 am at Blue Beach and all of the troops by 8:30 am.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Neither San Román at Blue Beach nor Erneido Oliva at Red Beach could communicate as all of the radios had been soaked in the water during the landings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The SU-100 from which Fidel Castro reportedly shelled the freighter Houston during the morning of 17 April." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg/220px-SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg/330px-SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg/440px-SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4160" data-file-height="2340" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SU_100_in_Havana_Cuba.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/SU-100" title="SU-100">SU-100</a> from which Fidel Castro reportedly shelled the freighter Houston during the morning of 17 April</div></div></div>
<p>At about 07:30, five <a href="/wiki/Curtiss_C-46_Commando" title="Curtiss C-46 Commando">C-46</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Douglas_C-54_Skymaster" title="Douglas C-54 Skymaster">C-54</a> transport aircraft dropped 177 paratroops from the parachute battalion of Brigade 2506 in an action code-named <i>Operation Falcon</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-cooper_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooper-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> About 30 men, plus heavy equipment, were dropped south of the Central <a href="/wiki/Australia,_Cuba" title="Australia, Cuba">Australia</a> sugar mill on the road to Palpite and Playa Larga, but the equipment was lost in the swamps, and the troops failed to block the road.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Other troops were dropped at <a href="/w/index.php?title=San_Blas,_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="San Blas, Cuba (page does not exist)">San Blas</a>, at Jocuma between Covadonga and San Blas, and at Horquitas between Yaguaramas and San Blas. Those positions to block the roads were maintained for two days, reinforced by ground troops from Playa Girón and tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999206_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999206-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The paratroopers had landed amid a collection of militia, but their training allowed them to hold their own against the ill-trained militiamen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> However, the dispersal of the paratroopers as they landed meant they were unable to take the road from the Central Australia sugar mill down to Playa Larga, which allowed the government to continue to send troops down to resist the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928_139-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200928-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 08:30, a FAR Sea Fury piloted by Carlos Ulloa Arauz crashed in the bay, due to stalling or anti-aircraft fire, after encountering a FAL C-46 returning south after dropping paratroops. By 09:00, Cuban troops and militia from outside the area had started arriving at the Central Australia sugar mill, Covadonga and Yaguaramas. Throughout the day they were reinforced by more troops, heavy armour and <a href="/wiki/T-34" title="T-34">T-34</a> tanks typically carried on flat-bed trucks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999195–96_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999195–96-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> At about 09:30, FAR <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Sea_Fury" title="Hawker Sea Fury">Sea Furies</a> and T-33s fired rockets at <i>Rio Escondido</i>, which then 'blew up' and sank about 3 kilometres (1.9&#160;mi) south of Girón.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <i>Rio Escondido</i> was loaded with aviation fuel and as the ship started to burn, the captain gave the order to abandon ship with the ship being destroyed in three explosions shortly afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <i>Rio Escondido</i> carried not only fuel, but also enough ammunition, food and medical supplies to last ten days and the radio that allowed the Brigade to communicate with the Liberation Air Force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The loss of the communications ship <i>Rio Escondido</i> meant that San Román was only able to issue orders to the forces at Blue Beach, and he had no idea of what was happening at Red Beach or with the paratroopers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> A messenger from Red Beach arrived at about 10:00 am asking San Román to send tank and infantry to block the road from the Central Australia sugar mill, a request that he agreed to.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929_143-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> It was not expected that government forces would be counter-attacking from this direction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929–30_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200929–30-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 11:00, Premier Fidel Castro issued a statement over Cuba's nationwide network saying that the invaders, members of the exiled Cuban revolutionary front, have come to destroy the revolution and take away the dignity and rights of men.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 11:00, a FAR T-33 attacked and shot down a FAL B-26 (serial number 935) piloted by Matias Farias, who then survived a crash landing on the Girón airfield, his navigator Eduardo González already killed by gunfire. His companion B-26 suffered damage and diverted to Grand Cayman Island; pilot Mario Zúñiga (the 'defector') and navigator Oscar Vega returned to Puerto Cabezas via CIA C-54 on 18 April. By about 11:00, the two remaining freighters <i>Caribe</i> and <i>Atlántico</i>, and the CIA LCIs and LCUs, started retreating south to international waters, but still pursued by FAR aircraft. At about noon, a FAR B-26 exploded due to heavy anti-aircraft fire from <i>Blagar</i>, and pilot Luis Silva Tablada (on his second sortie) and his crew of three were lost.<sup id="cite_ref-hagedorn2006_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hagedorn2006-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By noon, hundreds of Cuban militia cadets from Matanzas had secured Palpite and cautiously advanced on foot south towards Playa Larga, suffering many casualties during attacks by FAL B-26s. By dusk, other Cuban ground forces gradually advanced southward from Covadonga, southwest from Yaguaramas toward San Blas, and westward along coastal tracks from Cienfuegos towards Girón all without heavy weapons or armour.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> At 2:30 pm a group of militiamen from the 339th Battalion set up a position, which came under attack from the <i>brigadista</i> <a href="/wiki/M41_Walker_Bulldog" title="M41 Walker Bulldog">M41 Walker Bulldog</a> tanks, who inflicted heavy losses on the defenders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> This action is remembered in Cuba as the "Slaughter of the Lost Battalion" as most of the militiamen perished.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Three FAL B-26s were shot down by FAR T-33s, with the loss of pilots Raúl Vianello, José Crespo, Osvaldo Piedra and navigators Lorenzo Pérez-Lorenzo and José Fernández. Vianello's navigator Demetrio Pérez bailed out and was picked up by USS <i>Murray</i>. Pilot Crispín García Fernández and navigator Juan González Romero, in B-26 serial 940, diverted to Boca Chica, but late that night they attempted to fly back to Puerto Cabezas in B-26 serial 933 that Crespo had flown to Boca Chica on 15 April. In October 1961, the remains of the B-26 and its two crew were found in the dense jungle in Nicaragua.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> One FAL B-26 diverted to Grand Cayman with engine failure. By 16:00, Fidel Castro had arrived at the Central Australia sugar mill, joining <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ram%C3%B3n_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="José Ramón Fernández">José Ramón Fernández</a> whom he had appointed as battlefield commander before dawn that day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999168_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999168-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 April 1961, Osvaldo Ramírez (leader of the <a href="/wiki/War_Against_the_Bandits" class="mw-redirect" title="War Against the Bandits">rural resistance to Castro</a>) was captured by Castro's forces in Aromas de Velázquez, and immediately executed.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 21:00 on 17 April 1961, a night air strike by three FAL B-26s on San Antonio de Los Baños airfield failed, reportedly due to incompetence and bad weather. Two other B-26s had aborted the mission after take-off.<sup id="cite_ref-hagedorn2006_96-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hagedorn2006-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-macphall_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macphall-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Other sources allege that heavy anti-aircraft fire scared the aircrews<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> As night fell, <i>Atlantico</i> and <i>Caribe</i> pulled away from Cuba to be followed by <i>Blagar</i> and <i>Barbara J</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The ships were to return to the Bay of Pigs the following day to unload more ammunition, however the captains of the <i>Atlantico</i> and <i>Caribe</i> decided to abandon the invasion and head out to open sea fearing further air attacks by the FAR.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Destroyers from the US Navy intercepted <i>Atlantico</i> about 110 miles south of Cuba, and persuaded the captain to return, but <i>Caribe</i> was not intercepted until she was 218 miles away from Cuba, and she was not to return until it was too late.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Invasion_day_plus_one_.28D.2B1.29_18_April"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion_day_plus_one_(D+1)_18_April">Invasion day plus one (D+1) 18 April</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Invasion day plus one (D+1) 18 April">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the night of 17–18 April, the force at Red Beach came under repeated counter-attacks from the Cuban Army and militia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930–31_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930–31-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> As casualties mounted and ammunition was used up, the <i>brigadistas</i> steadily gave way.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930–31_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200930–31-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Airdrops from four C-54s and 2 C-46s had only limited success in landing more ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931_151-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Both the <i>Blagar</i> and <i>Barbara J</i> returned at midnight to land more ammunition, which proved insufficient for the <i>brigadistas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931_151-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200931-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Following desperate appeals for help from Oliva, San Román ordered all of his M41 tanks' six mortars to assist in the defense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> During the night fighting, a tank battle broke out when the <i>brigadista</i> M41 Walker Bulldog tanks clashed with the T-34 tanks of the Cuban Army. This sharp action drop back the <i>brigadistas.</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> At 8:00 pm, the Cuban Army opened fire with its 76.2mm and 122mm artillery guns on the <i>brigadista</i> forces at Playa Larga, which was followed by an attack by T-34 tanks at about midnight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The 2,000 artillery rounds fired by the Cuban Army had mostly missed the <i>brigadista</i> defense positions, and the T-34 tanks rode into an ambush when they came under fire from the <i>brigadista</i> M41 tanks and mortar fire, and a number of T-34 tanks were destroyed or knocked out.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> At 1:00 am, Cuban Army infantrymen and militiamen started an offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Despite heavy losses on the part of the Communist forces, the shortage of ammunition forced the <i>brigadistas</i> back and the T-34 tanks continued to force their way past the wreckage of the battlefield to press on the assault.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The Communist forces numbered about 2,100 consisting of about 300 FAR soldiers, 1,600 militiamen and 200 policemen supported by 20 T-34s who were faced by 370 <i>brigadistas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941_153-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> By 5:00 am, Oliva started to order his men to retreat as he had almost no ammunition or mortar rounds left.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941–42_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200941–42-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> By about 10:30 am on 18 April, Cuban troops and militia, supported by the T-34 tanks and 122mm artillery, took Playa Larga after Brigade forces had fled towards Girón in the early hours. During the day, Brigade forces retreated to San Blas along the two roads from Covadonga and Yaguaramas. By then, both Fidel Castro and José Ramón Fernández had re-located to that battlefront area.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999207_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999207-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the men from Red Beach arrived at Girón, San Román and Oliva met to discuss the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200942_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200942-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> With ammunition running low, Oliva suggested that the Brigade 2506 retreat into the Escambray mountains to wage guerilla warfare, but San Román decided to hold the beachhead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200942–43_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200942–43-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> At about 11:00 am, the Cuban government began an offensive to take San Blas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200943_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200943-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> San Román ordered all of the paratroopers back in order to hold San Blas, and they halted the offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200943_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200943-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> During the afternoon, Castro kept the <i>brigadistas</i> under steady air attack and artillery fire, but did not order any new major attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200943_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200943-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At 14:00, President Kennedy received a telegram from Nikita Khrushchev in Moscow, stating the Russians would not allow the US to enter Cuba, and implied swift nuclear retribution to the United States heartland if their warnings were not heeded.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 17:00 on 18 April, FAL B-26s attacked a Cuban column of 12 private buses leading trucks carrying tanks and other armor, moving southeast between Playa Larga and Punta Perdiz. The vehicles, loaded with civilians, militia, police, and soldiers, were attacked with bombs, <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a>, and rockets, suffering heavy casualties. The six B-26s were piloted by two CIA contract pilots plus four pilots and six navigators from Brigade 2506 air force.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The column later re-formed and advanced to Punta Perdiz, about 11&#160;km northwest of Girón.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999197_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez1999197-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Invasion_day_plus_two_.28D.2B2.29_19_April"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion_day_plus_two_(D+2)_19_April">Invasion day plus two (D+2) 19 April</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Invasion day plus two (D+2) 19 April">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_(CVS-9)_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_%28CVS-9%29_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg/220px-A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_%28CVS-9%29_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_%28CVS-9%29_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg/330px-A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_%28CVS-9%29_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_%28CVS-9%29_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg/440px-A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_%28CVS-9%29_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="353" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A4D-2_Skyhawks_of_VA-34_in_flight_over_USS_Essex_(CVS-9)_during_the_Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion_in_April_1961.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Douglas_A-4_Skyhawk" title="Douglas A-4 Skyhawk">Douglas A-4 Skyhawks</a> from the USS <i>Essex</i> flying sorties over combat areas during the invasion</div></div></div>
<p>During the night of 18 April, a FAL C-46 delivered arms and equipment to the Girón airstrip occupied by Brigade 2506 ground forces and took off before daybreak on 19 April.<sup id="cite_ref-frusx110_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frusx110-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The C-46 also evacuated Matias Farias, the pilot of B-26 serial '935' (code-named <i>Chico Two</i>) that had been shot down and crash-landed at Girón on 17 April.<sup id="cite_ref-cooper_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooper-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The crews of the <i>Barbara J</i> and <i>Blagar</i> had done their best to land what ammunition they had left onto the beachhead, but without air support the captains of both ships reported that it was too dangerous to be operating off the Cuban coast by day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200944_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200944-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The final air attack mission (code-named <i>Mad Dog Flight</i>) comprised five B-26s, four of which were manned by American CIA contract aircrews and volunteer pilots from the Alabama Air Guard. One FAR Sea Fury (piloted by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Douglas_Rudd&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Douglas Rudd (page does not exist)">Douglas Rudd</a>) and two FAR T-33s (piloted by Rafael del Pino and Alvaro Prendes) shot down two of these B-26s, killing four American airmen.<sup id="cite_ref-nsa_51-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Combat air patrols were flown by Douglas <a href="/wiki/A4D" class="mw-redirect" title="A4D">A4D-2N</a> Skyhawk jets of <a href="/wiki/VFA-34" title="VFA-34">VA-34</a> squadron operating from USS <i>Essex</i>, with nationality and other markings removed. Sorties were flown to reassure Brigade soldiers and pilots, and to intimidate Cuban government forces without directly engaging in acts of war.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> At 10 am, a tank battle had broken out, with the <i>brigadista</i> holding their line until about 2:00 pm, which led Olvia to order a retreat into Girón.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200945_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200945-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Following the last air attacks, San Román ordered his paratroopers and the men of the 3rd Battalion to launch a surprise attack, which was initially successful, but soon failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200945_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200945-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> With the <i>brigadistas</i> in disorganized retreat, the Cuban Army and militiamen started to advance rapidly, taking San Blas only to be stopped outside of Girón at about 11 am.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200945_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200945-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Later that afternoon, San Román heard the rumbling of the advancing T-34s and reported that with no more mortar rounds and bazooka rounds, he could not stop the tanks and ordered his men to fall back to the beach.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200946_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200946-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Oliva arrived afterward to find that the <i>brigadistas</i> were all heading out to the beach or retreating into the jungle or swamps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200946_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200946-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Without direct air support, and short of ammunition, Brigade 2506 ground forces retreated to the beaches in the face of a considerable onslaught from Cuban government artillery, tanks and infantry.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_93-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Late on 19 April, destroyers USS <i>Eaton</i> (code-named <i>Santiago</i>) and USS <i>Murray</i> (code-named <i>Tampico</i>) moved into Cochinos Bay to evacuate retreating Brigade soldiers from beaches, before fire from Cuban army tanks caused Commodore Crutchfield to order a withdrawal.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Invasion_day_plus_three_.28D.2B3.29_20_April"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion_day_plus_three_(D+3)_20_April">Invasion day plus three (D+3) 20 April</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Invasion day plus three (D+3) 20 April">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From 19 April until about 22 April, sorties were flown by A4D-2Ns to obtain visual intelligence over combat areas. Reconnaissance flights are also reported of Douglas <a href="/wiki/A-1_Skyraider" class="mw-redirect" title="A-1 Skyraider">AD-5Ws</a> of <a href="/wiki/VFP-62" title="VFP-62">VFP-62</a> and/or VAW-12 squadron from USS <i>Essex</i> or another carrier, such as USS <i>Shangri-La</i> that was part of the task force assembled off the Cayman Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_125-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 April, <i>Eaton</i> and <i>Murray</i>, joined on 22 April by destroyers USS <i>Conway</i> and USS <i>Cony</i>, plus submarine USS <i>Threadfin</i> and a CIA PBY-5A Catalina flying boat, continued to search the coastline, reefs, and islands for scattered Brigade survivors, about 24–30 being rescued.<sup id="cite_ref-frusx110_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frusx110-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties">Casualties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Casualties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>67 Cuban exiles from Brigade 2506 were killed in action plus ten on the firing squad, ten on the boat Celia trying to escape, 9 in the sealed container on the way to Havana, four by accident, 2 in prison, 4 Americans aviators for a total of 106 casualties.<sup id="ref_E_2" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_E">[E]</a></sup> Aircrews <a href="/wiki/Killed_in_action" title="Killed in action">killed in action</a> totaled six from the Cuban air force, 10 Cuban exiles and 4 American airmen.<sup id="cite_ref-hagedorn2006_96-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hagedorn2006-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>  Paratrooper Eugene Herman Koch<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> was killed in action, and the American airmen shot down were Thomas W. Ray, Leo F. Baker, Riley W. Shamburger, and Wade C. Gray.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In 1979, the body of Thomas 'Pete' Ray was repatriated from Cuba. In the 1990s, the CIA admitted he was linked to the agency, and awarded him the <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_Star" title="Intelligence Star">Intelligence Star</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The final toll in Cuban armed forces during the conflict was 176 killed in action.<sup id="ref_B_2" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_B">[B]</a></sup> This figure includes only the Cuban Army and it is estimated that about 2,000 militiamen were killed or wounded during the fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuesada200946_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuesada200946-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Other Cuban forces casualties were between 500 and 4,000 (killed, wounded or missing).<sup id="ref_C_2" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_C">[C]</a></sup> The airfield attacks on 15 April left 7 Cubans dead and 53 wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-fernandez_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fernandez-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Archive" title="National Security Archive">National Security Archive</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a>, released over 1200 pages of documents. One detail within these documents was incidents of friendly fire. The CIA had outfitted some B-26 bombers to appear as Cuban aircraft, having ordered them to remain inland to avoid being fired upon by American-backed forces. Some of the planes, not heeding the warning, came under fire. According to CIA operative Grayston Lynch, "we couldn't tell them from the Castro planes. We ended up shooting at two or three of them. We hit some of them there because when they came at us…it was a silhouette, that was all you could see."<sup id="cite_ref-nsarchive.gwu.edu_127-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsarchive.gwu.edu-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prisoners">Prisoners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Prisoners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Havana gleefully noted the wealth of the captured invaders: 100 plantation owners, 67 landlords of apartment houses, 35 factory owners, 112 businessmen, 179 lived off unearned income, and 194 ex-soldiers of Batista.</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>On 19 April 1961, at least seven Cubans plus two CIA-hired US citizens (Angus K. McNair and Howard F. Anderson) were executed in Pinar del Rio province, after a two-day trial. On 20 April, <a href="/wiki/Humberto_Sor%C3%AD_Marin" title="Humberto Sorí Marin">Humberto Sorí Marin</a> was executed at <a href="/wiki/La_Caba%C3%B1a" title="La Cabaña">Fortaleza de la Cabaña</a>, having been arrested on 18 March following infiltration into Cuba with 14 tons of explosives. His fellow conspirators Rogelio González Corzo (alias "Francisco Gutierrez"), Rafael Diaz Hanscom, Eufemio Fernandez, Arturo Hernandez Tellaheche and Manuel Lorenzo Puig Miyar were also executed.<sup id="cite_ref-corzo_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corzo-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thomas_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-triay_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-triay-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodríguez199946_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodríguez199946-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between April and October 1961, hundreds of executions took place in response to the invasion. They took place at various prisons, including the Fortaleza de la Cabaña and <a href="/wiki/Morro_Castle_(Havana)" title="Morro Castle (Havana)">Morro Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-triay_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-triay-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Infiltration team leaders Antonio Diaz Pou and Raimundo E. Lopez, as well as underground students Virgilio Campaneria, Alberto Tapia Ruano, and more than one hundred other insurgents were executed.<sup id="cite_ref-faria_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faria-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>About 1,202 members of Brigade 2506 were captured, of whom nine died from asphyxiation during transfer to Havana in a closed truck. In May 1961, Fidel Castro proposed to exchange the surviving Brigade prisoners for 500 large farm tractors, valued at <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>28,000,000.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> On 8 September 1961, 14 Brigade prisoners were convicted of torture, murder and other major crimes committed in Cuba before the invasion. Five were executed and nine others imprisoned for 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-szulc1986_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-szulc1986-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Three confirmed as executed were Ramon Calvino, Emilio Soler Puig ("El Muerte") and Jorge King Yun ("El Chino").<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnson_93-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> On 29 March 1962, 1,179 men were put on trial for treason. On 7 April 1962, all were convicted and sentenced to 30 years in prison. On 14 April 1962, 60 wounded and sick prisoners were freed and transported to the US.<sup id="cite_ref-szulc1986_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-szulc1986-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 December 1962, Cuban Prime Minister Fidel Castro and <a href="/wiki/James_B._Donovan" title="James B. Donovan">James B. Donovan</a>, a US lawyer aided by <a href="/wiki/Milan_C._Miskovsky" title="Milan C. Miskovsky">Milan C. Miskovsky</a>, a CIA legal officer,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> signed an agreement to exchange 1,113 prisoners for US$53 million in food and medicine, sourced from private donations and from companies expecting tax concessions. On 24 December 1962, some prisoners were flown to Miami, others following on the ship <i>African Pilot</i>, plus about 1,000 family members also allowed to leave Cuba. On 29 December 1962, President Kennedy and his wife Jacqueline attended a "welcome back" ceremony for Brigade 2506 veterans at the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Bowl_(game)" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange Bowl (game)">Orange Bowl</a> in <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_93-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_reaction">Political reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Political reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20,_1961.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20%2C_1961.png/220px-Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20%2C_1961.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20%2C_1961.png/330px-Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20%2C_1961.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20%2C_1961.png/440px-Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20%2C_1961.png 2x" data-file-width="2209" data-file-height="2753" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_F._Kennedy_Statement_on_Cuba_and_Neutrality_Laws_April_20,_1961.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>'s Statement on Cuba and Neutrality Laws, 20 April 1961</div></div></div>
<p>The failed invasion severely embarrassed the <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy administration</a>, and made Castro wary of future US intervention in Cuba. On 21 April, in a State Department press conference, President Kennedy said: "There's an old saying that victory has a hundred fathers and defeat is an orphan... Further statements, detailed discussions, are not to conceal responsibility because I'm the responsible officer of the Government..."<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The initial U.S. response concerning the first air attacks was of a dismissive quality. U.S. Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a> denied any involvement in the first wave of air strikes, stating before the United Nations, "These charges are totally false and I deny them categorically." Stevenson continued to promote a story of two Cuban planes that had reportedly defected to the United States, apparently unaware that they were in fact U.S. planes piloted by U.S.-backed Cuban pilots to promote a false story of defection.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1961, during an economic conference of the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">Organization of American States</a> in <a href="/wiki/Punta_del_Este" title="Punta del Este">Punta del Este</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a> sent a note to Kennedy via <a href="/wiki/Richard_N._Goodwin" title="Richard N. Goodwin">Richard N. Goodwin</a>, a secretary of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. It read: "Thanks for Playa Girón. Before the invasion, the revolution was weak. Now it's stronger than ever".<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, Guevara answered a set of questions from <a href="/wiki/Leo_Huberman" title="Leo Huberman">Leo Huberman</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Monthly_Review" title="Monthly Review">Monthly Review</a></i> following the invasion. In one reply, Guevara was asked to explain the growing number of Cuban <a href="/wiki/Counter-revolutionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter-revolutionaries">counter-revolutionaries</a> and defectors from the regime, to which he replied that the repelled invasion was the climax of counter-revolution, and that afterward such actions "fell drastically to zero." Regarding the defections of some prominent figures within the Cuban government, Guevara remarked that this was because "the socialist revolution left the opportunists, the ambitious, and the fearful far behind and now advances toward a new regime free of this class of vermin."<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Allen Dulles later stated, CIA planners believed that once the troops were on the ground, Kennedy would authorize any action required to prevent failure – as Eisenhower had done in Guatemala in 1954 after that invasion looked as if it would collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> President Kennedy was deeply depressed and angered with the failure.  Several years after his death, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported that he told an unspecified high administration official of wanting "to splinter the CIA in a thousand pieces and scatter it to the winds." However, following a "rigorous inquiry into the agency's affairs, methods, and problems... [Kennedy] did not 'splinter' it after all and did not recommend Congressional supervision."<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy commented to his journalist friend <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_C._Bradlee" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin C. Bradlee">Ben Bradlee</a>, "The first advice I'm going to give my successor is to watch the generals and to avoid feeling that because they were military men their opinions on military matters were worth a damn."<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cuban_missiles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Aerial view of missile launch site at San Cristobal, Cuba" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cuban_missiles.jpg/220px-Cuban_missiles.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cuban_missiles.jpg/330px-Cuban_missiles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cuban_missiles.jpg/440px-Cuban_missiles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1160" data-file-height="1121" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cuban_missiles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of missile launch site at San Cristobal, Cuba<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The aftermath of the Bay of Pigs invasion and events involving Cuba that followed caused the US to feel threatened by their neighbor.  Previous to the events at <a href="/wiki/Playa_Gir%C3%B3n" title="Playa Girón">Playa Girón</a> the US government imposed embargoes that limited trade with Cuba. An article appearing in <i>The New York Times</i> dated 6 January 1960 called trade with Cuba "too risky."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> About six months later in July 1960, the US reduced the import quota of Cuban sugar, leaving the US to increase its sugar supply using other sources.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Immediately following the Bay of Pigs invasion, the Kennedy Administration considered complete trade restrictions with Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Five months later the president was authorized to do so. After Cuba's declaration of Marxism, the Kennedy administration imposed a complete trade embargo against Cuba. After the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> in 1962 the Kennedy Administration imposed strict travel restrictions for U.S. citizens.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>According to author Jim Rasenberger, the Kennedy administration became very aggressive in regards to overthrowing Fidel Castro following the failure of the Bay of Pigs Invasion, reportedly doubling its efforts against Castro. Rasenberger elaborated on the fact that almost every decision that was made by Kennedy following the Bay of Pigs had some correlation with the destruction of the Castro administration. Shortly after the invasion ended, Kennedy ordered the Pentagon to design secret operations to overthrow the Castro regime. Also, President Kennedy persuaded his brother Robert to set up a covert action against Castro which was known as "Operation Mongoose." This clandestine operation included sabotage and assassination plots.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_analysis">Later analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Later analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maxwell_Taylor_survey">Maxwell Taylor survey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Maxwell Taylor survey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 22 April 1961, President Kennedy asked General <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_D._Taylor" title="Maxwell D. Taylor">Maxwell D. Taylor</a>, Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Arleigh_Burke" title="Arleigh Burke">Arleigh Burke</a> and CIA Director Allen Dulles to form the Cuba Study Group, to report on lessons to learn from the failed operation.  General Taylor submitted the Board of Inquiry's report to President Kennedy on 13 June.  It attributed the defeat to lack of early realization of the impossibility of success by covert means, to inadequate aircraft, to limitations on armaments, pilots, and air attacks set to attempt <a href="/wiki/Plausible_deniability" title="Plausible deniability">plausible deniability</a> – and, ultimately, to loss of important ships and lack of ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Taylor Commission was criticized, and bias implied.  Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert Kennedy</a> was included in the group, and the commission collectively was seen to be more preoccupied with deflecting blame from the White House than concerned with realizing the real depth of mistakes that promoted the failure in Cuba.
</p><p>Jack Pfeiffer, who worked as a historian for the CIA until the mid-1980s, simplified his own view of the failed Bay of Pigs effort by quoting a statement which Raúl Castro, Fidel's brother, had made to a Mexican journalist in 1975:  "Kennedy vacillated," Raúl Castro said.  "If at that moment he had decided to invade us, he could have suffocated the island in a sea of blood, but he could have destroyed the revolution.  Lucky for us, he vacillated."<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="C.I.A._report">C.I.A. report</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: C.I.A. report">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="CIA cover report Bay of Pigs.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg/220px-CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg/330px-CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CIA_cover_report_Bay_of_Pigs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>In November 1961, CIA Inspector-General <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Kirkpatrick" title="Lyman Kirkpatrick">Lyman B Kirkpatrick</a>,
authored a report 'Survey of the Cuban Operation,' that remained classified until 1998. Conclusions were:<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The C.I.A. exceeded its capabilities in developing the project from guerrilla support to overt armed action without any plausible deniability.</li>
<li>Failure to realistically assess risks and to adequately communicate information and decisions internally and with other government principals.</li>
<li>Insufficient involvement of leaders of the exiles.</li>
<li>Failure to sufficiently organize internal resistance in Cuba.</li>
<li>Failure to competently collect and analyze intelligence about Cuban forces.</li>
<li>Poor internal management of communications and staff.</li>
<li>Insufficient employment of high-quality staff.</li>
<li>Insufficient Spanish-speakers, training facilities, and material resources.</li>
<li>Lack of stable policies and/or contingency plans.</li></ol>
<p>In spite of vigorous rebuttals by CIA management of the findings, CIA Director <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">Allen Dulles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Director_of_Central_Intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Director of Central Intelligence">CIA Deputy Director</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cabell" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Cabell">Charles Cabell</a>, and Deputy Director for Plans <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Bissell,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Richard M. Bissell, Jr.">Richard Bissell</a> were all forced to resign by early 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-higgins_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-higgins-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In later years, the CIA's behavior in the event became the prime example cited for the psychology paradigm known as <a href="/wiki/Groupthink" title="Groupthink">groupthink</a> syndrome.<sup id="cite_ref-wyden_104-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wyden-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further study shows that among various components of groupthink analyzed by <a href="/wiki/Irving_Janis" title="Irving Janis">Irving Janis</a>, The Bay of Pigs Invasion followed the structural characteristics that led to an irrational decision making in foreign policy pushed by deficiency in impartial leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> An account on the process of invasion decision reads,<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> At each meeting, instead of opening up the agenda to permit a full airing of the opposing considerations, [President Kennedy] allowed the CIA representatives to dominate the entire discussion. The president permitted them to refute each tentative doubt immediately that one of the others might express, instead of asking whether anyone else had the same doubt or wanted to pursue the implications of the new worrisome issue that had been raised.
</p></blockquote>
